Imam al-Sanusi's Vast Knowledge
Testimonies to al-Sanusi's scholarly breadth from two scholars.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam al-Sanusi's scholarly output and the testimonies of later scholars establish his reputation for vast knowledge, especially in tawhid and tasawwuf.

Core Idea
Al-Sanusi's knowledge was so vast that scholars called him a 'wonder' and a renewer of religion.

This lesson presents biographical evidence for Imam al-Sanusi's immense learning. Rather than listing his works, it lets two eminent scholars testify to his mastery in both exoteric and esoteric sciences, particularly tawhid and tasawwuf. Their words help us appreciate his standing as a mujaddid and a saint.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the two scholars quoted
Find the names al-Shifshāwūnī and al-Ḥaḍīkī. Their testimonies form the core of this passage.
These are two later scholars who praised al-Sanusi.
2
Notice how al-Shifshāwūnī connects al-Sanusi to the hadith of the mujaddid
He says al-Sanusi renewed religion at the turn of the ninth century, linking him to the prophetic tradition about a renewer every century.
This elevates al-Sanusi's status significantly.
3
See how al-Ḥaḍīkī emphasizes al-Sanusi's uniqueness in esoteric science
He calls him an 'āyah (wonder) and says he was unique in ʿilm al-bāṭin, the esoteric knowledge that leads to the sciences of the afterlife.
His exoteric studies always pointed to deeper spiritual truths.
4
Reflect on what the passage says about the relationship between exoteric and esoteric knowledge
Al-Ḥaḍīkī states that al-Sanusi would not read anything of exoteric science without it leading to the sciences of the afterlife, showing integration.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما سعة علمه : فآثاره العلمية الواسعة تشهد له بذلك ، ولا سيما علم التوحيد والتصوف قال فيه العلامة الشفشاوني : ( كان من مشايخ المئة التاسعة ، وتوفي على رأسها ، فكان من جدَّد لهذه الأمة أمر دينها على رأس تلك المئة كما أخبر الصادق المصدوق صلى الله عليه وسلم ، وكان من أكابر الأولياء ، وأعلام العلماء ، وتآليفه تدلُّ على تحقيقه وغزارة علمه ) (۱) وقال العلامة الحضيكي : ( كان آية في العلم والصلاح والهدى والزهد والورع ، له أوفر حظ في العلوم فروعها وأصولها ، إذا تحدث في علم ظنَّ السامع أنه لا يحسن غيره ، لا سيما في التوحيد ، وانفرد بعلم الباطن ، لا يقرأ شيئاً من علم الظاهر إلا خرج لعلوم الآخرة ، (۱)
As for the vastness of his knowledge: his extensive scholarly works testify to that, especially the sciences of tawḥīd (monotheism) and taṣawwuf (Sufism). The scholar al-Shifshāwūnī said about him: 'He was among the masters of the ninth century, and he died at its beginning, so he was among those who renewed for this nation the matters of its religion at the head of that century, as the truthful and believed (ṣādiq al-maṣdūq) — may God bless him and grant him peace — informed, and he was among the greatest saints and the eminent scholars, and his writings indicate his verification and abundance of knowledge.' And the scholar al-Ḥaḍīkī said: 'He was a wonder in knowledge, righteousness, guidance, asceticism, and piety; he had the greatest share in the sciences, its branches and its roots; if he spoke about a science, the listener would think he knew nothing else, especially in tawḥīd; and he was unique in the esoteric science (ʿilm al-bāṭin); he would not read anything of exoteric science except that it would lead to the sciences of the afterlife.'
Source pages p. 39

Commentary
This passage is a testimony to Imam al-Sanusi's immense knowledge. It begins with a general statement that his many scholarly works prove his erudition, especially in tawḥīd (theology) and taṣawwuf (mysticism). Then two scholars give specific praise. Al-Shifshāwūnī places him among the masters of the ninth Islamic century, who died at its onset, and identifies him as a renewer (mujaddid) of religion at the turn of that century, in accordance with a prophetic hadith. He also calls him one of the greatest saints (akābir al-awliyāʾ) and eminent scholars (aʿlām al-ʿulamāʾ), stating that his writings show his verification (taḥqīq) and abundant knowledge. Al-Ḥaḍīkī goes further: al-Sanusi is an 'āyah (wonder, sign) in knowledge, righteousness (ṣalāḥ), guidance (hudā), asceticism (zuhd), and piety (waraʿ). He has the greatest share in all sciences, both branches and roots. When he speaks on any science, the listener thinks he knows nothing else, especially in tawḥīd. Moreover, he was unique in esoteric knowledge (ʿilm al-bāṭin), and his study of exoteric knowledge always led to the sciences of the afterlife, showing his spiritual orientation.

Testimonies to the Scholar's Vast Knowledge
Documents the scholar's vast knowledge through testimonies and his own definition of a true scholar.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how testimonies from contemporaries and the scholar's own definition establish his vast knowledge and the criteria for a true scholar.

Core Idea
A true scholar is one who sees problems in what is clear and clarifies what is ambiguous.

This lesson presents three testimonies that demonstrate the scholar's vast knowledge, especially in tawhid and Sufism. It also includes the scholar's own definition of a true scholar, which emphasizes deep understanding and the ability to clarify. After reading these accounts, you will appreciate the high regard in which the scholar was held and the standard he set for true scholarship.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it, then read the literal translation. Let the testimonies and definition sink in.
2
Identify the three testimonies
Find the testimonies of al-Shafshawani, al-Hadiki, and Ibn Maryam. Notice what each emphasizes about the scholar's knowledge.
Al-Shafshawani calls him a mujaddid (renewer).
3
Study the scholar's own definition
Read carefully the definition of the true scholar. Reflect on why he considers finding the clear problematic and clarifying the ambiguous as key signs.
This definition is the core of the lesson.
4
Relate testimonies to definition
Consider how the testimonies exemplify the definition. For instance, al-Hadiki's description of the scholar's insight into both exoteric and esoteric sciences shows his ability to 'clarify the ambiguous.'

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما سعة علمه : فآثاره العلمية الواسعة تشهد له بذلك ، ولا سيما علم التوحيد والتصوف قال فيه العلامة الشفشاوني : ( كان من مشايخ المئة التاسعة ، وتوفي على رأسها ، فكان من جدَّد لهذه الأمة أمر دينها على رأس تلك المئة كما أخبر الصادق المصدوق صلى الله عليه وسلم ، وكان من أكابر الأولياء ، وأعلام العلماء ، وتآليفه تدلُّ على تحقيقه وغزارة علمه ((۱) وقال العلامة الحضيكي : ( كان آية في العلم والصلاح والهدى والزهد والورع ، له أوفر حظ في العلوم فروعها وأصولها ، إذا تحدث في علم ظنَّ السامع أنه لا يحسن غيره ، لا سيما في التوحيد ، وانفرد بعلم الباطن ، لا يقرأ شيئاً من علم الظاهر إلا خرج لعلوم الآخرة ، لا سيما في التفسير والحديث ، كأنه يشاهد الآخرة ؛ لكثرة مراقبته لله تعالى ((۱) قد جعل رحمه الله تعالى للعالم علامات يعرف بها ؛ حيث قال : ) العالم حقاً : من يستشكل الواضح ، ويوضح المشكل ؛ لسعة فهمه وعلمه وتحقيقه ، فهو الذي يُحضر مجلسه ، ويُستمع فوائده )(۲) >> وقال العلامة ابن مريم في البستان » : ( أما علومه الظاهرة فله فيها أوفر نصيب، وجمع مع من فروعها وأصولها السهم والتعصيب ((٣) .
As for the vastness of his knowledge: his extensive scholarly works testify to that, especially the sciences of divine unity and Sufism. The scholar al-Shafshawani said about him: 'He was among the shaykhs of the ninth century, and died at its beginning; he was one who renewed for this nation the matter of its religion at the turn of that century, as the truthful and believed (peace be upon him) informed. He was among the greatest saints and most prominent scholars. His writings indicate his verification and abundant knowledge.' (1) And the scholar al-Hadiki said: 'He was a wonder in knowledge, righteousness, guidance, asceticism, and piety. He had the greatest share in the sciences, both branches and roots. When he spoke about a science, the listener would think he knew nothing else, especially in tawhid (divine unity). He was unique in the esoteric science. He would not read anything of the exoteric science without extracting from it the sciences of the afterlife, especially in Tafsir and Hadith. It was as if he witnessed the afterlife due to his intense watchfulness of Allah Almighty.' (1) He (may Allah have mercy on him) made for the scholar signs by which he is known. He said: 'The true scholar is one who finds the clear problematic and clarifies the problematic, due to the breadth of his understanding, knowledge, and verification. So he is the one whose gathering is attended and whose benefits are listened to.' (2) And the scholar Ibn Maryam said in al-Bustan: 'As for his exoteric sciences, he had the greatest share in them, and he combined with their branches and roots both arrow and strong part.' (3)
Source pages p. 39

Commentary
This passage provides evidence of the scholar's vast knowledge through three testimonies. First, al-Shafshawani declares him a renewer (mujaddid) of the ninth century, indicating his high rank. Second, al-Hadiki emphasizes his mastery across all sciences, especially tawhid, and his unique combination of exoteric and esoteric knowledge, even claiming that his study of exoteric sciences always led to insights about the afterlife. Third, the scholar himself defines a true scholar as one who can interrogate clear matters and clarify ambiguous ones, showing his deep understanding. Finally, Ibn Maryam confirms his outstanding share in exoteric sciences. The passage highlights the scholar's comprehensive and transformative knowledge.

Key Terms
علم التوحيد
التصوف
المجدد
العالم الحق
علم الباطن
علم الظاهر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A true scholar is someone who has memorized many books or knows many facts.
Correction: The scholar defines a true scholar as one who can find problems in what is clear and clarify what is ambiguous, indicating deep analytical ability and understanding, not mere accumulation.

Checkpoint
According to the scholar's own definition in this passage, what is the true scholar?
A) One who only studies esoteric sciences
B) One who finds the clear problematic and clarifies the problematic
C) One who knows all branches of knowledge
D) One who is a saint (wali)

Key Takeaway
A true scholar is defined by his ability to find issues in clear matters and clarify ambiguity, as exemplified by the scholar's vast knowledge attested by his works and contemporaries.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you seek to be the kind of scholar who finds problems in what everyone considers clear, or do you settle for surface-level knowledge? How can you start training your mind to question and clarify today?
?
When you study a verse or a hadith, do you stop to extract lessons for the afterlife as this scholar did, or do you treat it as mere information? What is one practical step you can take to turn your learning into worship?

The True Saint's Asceticism: Turning Away Even from Paradise
Definition of a true saint by his asceticism

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the scholar's definition of a true saint as one who abstains even from paradise, and how this reflects ultimate asceticism.

Core Idea
The true saint turns away even from paradise to focus solely on Allah.

This passage presents a profound definition of sainthood from the scholar. It goes beyond ordinary asceticism—even the greatest reward does not distract a true saint from Allah. Let's examine his exact words and the lesson they teach.

Guided Path
1
Read the scholar's statement
Read the Arabic quote carefully: 'The reality of the knowledgeable saint: if paradise and its houris were unveiled to him... he would not turn to them, nor incline to other than Him, the Exalted.'
Notice the emphasis on 'not turning' even to paradise.
2
Understand the definition
Ask yourself why paradise is mentioned. The saint's detachment is so complete that even the highest reward does not capture his attention.
3
Reflect on the author's commentary
The author comments: 'So whoever abstains from such things is even more ascetic regarding what is lesser than it.' This means if he turns away from paradise, he certainly turns away from worldly things.
This shows the principle: greater asceticism implies lesser.
4
Consider the historical context
The passage mentions the Sultan trying to take from the school's revenues. This shows the saint's detachment was tested in practical matters.
The exact outcome is not given, but the reference is provided.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما زهده وورعه : فحسبك بكلمته التي تنوقلت في ترجمته ؛ حيث قال : ) حقيقة الولي العارف : من لو كشف له عن الجنة وحورها . . ما التفت إليها ، ولا ركن لغيره تعالى ((٤) ، فمن زهد بمثل هذا فهو عما دونه أزهد. وقد بعث إليه السلطان في أخذ شيء من غلات مدرسة الولي (۱) انظر ( طبقات الحضيكي » ( ص ٢٣٦ ) ، و ( نيل الابتهاج » ( ص ٥٦٦ ) .
And as for his asceticism and piety: your suffice is his statement that was transmitted in his biography: 'The reality of the knowledgeable saint is: if paradise and its houris were unveiled to him... he would not turn to them, nor incline to other than Him, the Exalted.' (4) So whoever abstains from such things is even more ascetic regarding what is lesser than it. And the Sultan sent to him to take something from the revenues of the school of the saint. (1) See: Tabaqat al-Hadiki, p. 236, and Nayl al-Ibtihaj, p. 566.
Source pages p. 40

Commentary
This passage discusses the scholar's asceticism. It begins by quoting his own definition of a true saint: one who, even if paradise and its houris were shown, would not turn to them or rely on anything other than God. This demonstrates ultimate detachment from worldly and even otherworldly desires. The author then comments that if he is ascetic towards paradise, he is even more so towards lesser things. The passage also mentions that the Sultan sought to take something from the school's revenues, but the text does not detail the outcome, only provides a reference. This section underscores his profound abstinence.

Correction: The scholar defines a saint as one who turns away even from paradise, showing detachment from all but Allah, not just from worldly things.

According to the scholar's statement, what would a true saint do if paradise and its houris were unveiled to him?
A) He would enter immediately
B) He would not turn to them nor incline to anything other than God
C) He would ask for more
D) He would wait until the Last Day

Key Takeaway
The true saint is not even distracted by the promise of paradise; his entire being is oriented towards Allah alone.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you were shown paradise in all its beauty, would you be able to turn away from it? What does your honest answer reveal about what your heart truly seeks?
?
Think of a religious act you do that you are attached to—like a certain prayer, a class, or a charitable project. Could you abandon it if it distracted you from Allah? What is your real attachment: the deed or the One you do it for?
?
The saint 'did not incline to other than Him.' When you wake up, where do your first thoughts incline? What worldly or otherworldly reward do you subconsciously rely on more than Allah Himself?

Life of Imam al-Sanusi
beginner

Aversion to the Worldly Elite
Narrative of Sanusi's aversion to worldly people and a specific incident.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Sanusi's asceticism manifested in his extreme avoidance of worldly people and authorities.

Core Idea
Sanusi's piety demanded complete detachment from worldly power and status.

Imam Sanusi's asceticism was not merely a passive inclination but an active, disciplined avoidance of worldly associations. This lesson explores his general attitude and a vivid anecdote demonstrating his measures to avoid contact with the sultan's elite.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on his general avoidance
Read the Arabic and translation describing Sanusi's feelings of unease with people and his hatred of worldly individuals.
Notice the intensity: he 'detested' associating with them.
2
Analyze the desert horsemen story
Read the story from his student al-Malali. Notice how Sanusi turns away or covers his face to avoid being seen.
This shows physical avoidance as part of his piety.
3
Reflect on the motivation
Consider why he did this. The passage frames it as part of his fear of God and detachment.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الصالح الحسن أبركان [؟]، فامتنع، فألحوا عليه، فكتب في الاعتذار وكتابة مطولة، فقبل منه. كان لا يأنس بأحدٍ ولا يتسبب في معرفته، وقد تبغض إليه الاجتماع بأهل الدنيا والنظر إليهم وقربهم، وهو لمن بسط لهم فيها ونالوا رتبها .. أشدُّ بغضاً، قال تلميذه الملالي: (خرج يوماً معنا للصحراء، فرأى فرساناً بثياب فاخرة على بعد، فقال: من هؤلاء؟ قلنا: خواص السلطان، فتعوذ ورجع لطريق آخر ولقيهم مرة أخرى، وما تمكن من الرجوع، فجعل وجهه للحائط وغطاه حتى جازوا ولم يروه). ولما أراد ختم التفسير عزم على قراءة سورة (الإخلاص) يوماً و(المعوذتين) يوماً، فسمع به الوزير وأراد حضور الختم فبلغه فقرأ السور الثلاثة يوماً واحداً؛ خيفة حضور الوزير عنده ذلك، ورؤيته والاجتماع به. وطلبه السلطان أن يطلع إليه ويقرأ التفسير بحضرته على عادة المفسرين، فامتنع، فألحوا عليه، فكتب إليه معتذراً بغلبة الحياء له، ولا يقدر على التكلم هناك، فأيسوا منه. وإذا سمع يوماً بوليمة أحد أبناء الدنيا تخلف من يومه عن الحضور؛ خيفة أن يُدعى، فلا يظهرُ بالكلية حتى تمر أيام الوليمة وربما تخلف قبله أياماً. وحاشى نفسه عن قبول عطايا السلطان ومن لاذ به، وربما تأتي داره وهو غائب، فإذا وجدها أنكر على أهل داره، وتغيَّر كثيراً، وكان يقبل هدايا غيرهم ممَّن طابت أكسابهم، ويدعو لهم. وقد جاءه يوماً ابن الخليفة ومعه عبد، فأقبل يقبل يديه ورجليه، وطلب منه قبوله هدية، فأبى، ثم تبسم مطيِّباً قلبه ودعا له، فطلب منه أن يأخذه ويتصدق به على الفقراء، فأبى ولم يرضَ لهم ذلك. ولعله كان يُقصد بشفاعات وقضاء حوائج، ويُطلب منه الكتابة إلى الأمراء، فكان يكره ذلك، وإن فعل فعن حياء؛ فقد كلَّفه إنسان يوماً بكتب ثلاثين براءة، فكتبها وقال: هذه مصائب ابتلينا بها. وكان يتمنَّى ألا يرى أحداً وألا يراه أحدٌ، وكان يقول: لا حاجة لي بأحد ولا بماله. وقد قال فيه العلامة الحوضي: (من الكامل) كم جاءَتِ الدنيا تسوقُ رئاسة يبغي إليك تقـربـاً أبناؤهــا فأبيت عنها معرضاً مستحقراً لم يخدعَنْكَ جمالها وبهاؤها. وأما عبادته وتبتله وخوفه من الله تعالى، وحسن معاملته مع عباده:
Al-Salih al-Hasan Abrakan [؟], so he refused, but they insisted, so he wrote in apology and a lengthy writing, and he accepted from him. He did not feel at ease with anyone and did not cause himself to be known, and he detested associating with people of the world, looking at them, and being near them, and he hated more intensely those who were given worldly goods and attained high ranks. His student al-Malali said: 'One day he went out with us to the desert, and he saw horsemen in luxurious clothing in the distance, and said: Who are these? We said: The sultan's elite. So he sought refuge and returned by another path. He encountered them another time and was not able to turn back, so he turned his face to the wall and covered it until they passed and did not see him.' And when he wanted to complete the tafsir, he resolved to recite Surah al-Ikhlas one day and al-Mu'awwidhatayn another day, but the minister heard of it and wanted to attend the completion ceremony, so when he learned of it, he recited the three surahs in one day, fearing the minister's presence, seeing him, and meeting with him. And the sultan requested that he come to him and recite the tafsir in his presence according to the custom of the exegetes, but he refused, and they insisted, so he wrote to him apologizing that shyness overcame him and he could not speak there, so they despaired of him. And if he heard one day of a wedding feast of one of the worldly people, he would stay away from attending that day, fearing that he might be invited, so he would not appear at all until the days of the feast passed, and sometimes he would stay away days before it. And he refrained from accepting gifts from the sultan and those who sought refuge with him, and sometimes gifts would come to his house while he was absent, and if he found them, he would rebuke his family and become very upset, but he would accept gifts from others whose earnings were lawful, and he would pray for them. And one day the son of the caliph came to him with a slave, and he began kissing his hands and feet, and asked him to accept a gift, but he refused, then he smiled, comforting his heart, and prayed for him, so he asked him to take it and give it in charity to the poor, but he refused and did not accept that for them. And perhaps he was sought for intercessions and fulfilling needs, and asked to write to the amirs, and he disliked that, and if he did it, it was out of shyness; for one day a man tasked him with writing thirty letters of acquittal, so he wrote them and said: These are calamities we have been afflicted with. And he wished that no one would see him and that he would see no one, and he used to say: I have no need of anyone or his wealth. And the scholar al-Hawdi said about him (in the meter kamil): How often the world came, bringing leadership, its sons seeking to draw near to you, but you refused it, turning away, holding it in contempt, its beauty and splendor did not deceive you. And as for his worship, his devotion, his fear of Allah the Exalted, and his good conduct with His servants:
Source pages p. 41

Commentary
This passage is a biographical account of Imam Sanusi's extraordinary asceticism and piety. It proceeds through a series of anecdotes and observations that illustrate his complete detachment from worldly status, power, and social interaction.

The text begins by recounting how he refused something (possibly a request), but after persistent insistence, he wrote a lengthy apology, which was accepted. This sets the tone for his reluctance to engage.

The narrative then states that he did not feel at ease with anyone and actively avoided being known. He detested associating with worldly people, especially those who had attained high ranks or wealth. This is a central theme: his aversion to the powerful and wealthy.

A specific story from his student al-Malali illustrates this: during a desert outing, they saw distant horsemen in fine clothes. When told they were the sultan's elite, Sanusi sought refuge (with God) and turned away. On another occasion, when escape was impossible, he turned his face to the wall and covered it until they passed. This shows his extreme measures to avoid contact with worldly authorities.

Another anecdote involves his completion of a tafsir (Quranic exegesis). He planned to recite Surah al-Ikhlas one day and al-Mu'awwidhatayn another day. When the minister heard and wanted to attend, Sanusi changed his plan and recited all three in one day to avoid the minister's presence. This highlights his fear of association with officials.

The sultan himself requested Sanusi to come and recite the tafsir in his presence, as was customary. Sanusi refused, and despite insistence, he wrote an apology pleading overwhelming shyness. They despaired and left him alone.

His avoidance extended to social events: if he heard of a wedding feast of a worldly person, he would stay away for that day and sometimes distant days, lest he be invited and forced to attend.

Regarding gifts, he absolutely refused those from the sultan or those associated with him. If gifts came to his house in his absence, he would rebuke his family. He accepted gifts only from those with lawful earnings, and he would pray for them. This shows his scrupulousness about the source of wealth.

A remarkable incident: the son of the caliph came with a slave, kissed his hands and feet, and offered a gift. Sanusi refused, then smiled and prayed for him. When asked to take it and give it to the poor, he still refused. This shows his refusal even of charity from such sources.

He was often sought for intercession and writing to amirs. He disliked this, but sometimes did it out of shyness. Once, a man asked him to write thirty letters of acquittal; he wrote them but lamented, 'These are calamities we have been afflicted with.'

His ultimate wish was to see no one and be seen by no one. He said, 'I have no need of anyone or his wealth.'

The passage ends with a poetic tribute by al-Hawdi, praising his rejection of worldly leadership and its allure.

Finally, the text transitions to his worship, devotion, fear of God, and good conduct, but this part is incomplete in our window. The entire passage serves to paint a picture of a scholar who embodied complete ascetic detachment.

Key Terms
الزهد
الحياء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That Sanusi's avoidance was due to misanthropy or social anxiety.
Correction: The passage shows it was a deliberate act of piety and detachment from worldly influence, not dislike of people.

Checkpoint
What did Imam Sanusi do when he encountered the sultan's elite horsemen and could not escape?
A) He greeted them respectfully.
B) He turned his face to the wall and covered it.
C) He ran away quickly.
D) He asked them for a favor.

Key Takeaway
Imam Sanusi's asceticism drove him to physically avoid worldly people, especially those in power.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter people who are wealthy or powerful, does your heart incline toward them or do you feel a sense of caution? What does that reveal about your attachment to this world?
?
Think of a recent situation where you could have avoided a potentially harmful environment but chose to stay. What held you back, and how might Sanusi's example challenge you to prioritize your faith next time?
?
If you were forced to be in the presence of those who flaunt worldly status, would you be able to physically turn away as Sanusi did, or would your nafs desire their approval? What step will you take today to strengthen your detachment?

Avoiding Authority and Social Events
Anecdotes of Sanusi avoiding authority figures and social events.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi avoided social and political engagements, including changing his plans to avoid the minister and staying away from wedding feasts.

Core Idea
For Sanusi, even religious gatherings were avoided if they involved worldly officials.

Sanusi's asceticism extended to his scholarly activities and social life. This lesson presents incidents where he altered his tafsir completion to avoid the minister, refused the sultan's invitation, and avoided wedding feasts of worldly people.

Guided Path

This shows his fear of association with authority.

His shyness is a pious reluctance, not mere timidity.

He would stay away for days to avoid being invited to worldly celebrations.

The narrative then states that he did not feel at ease with anyone and actively avoided being known. He detested associating with worldly people, especially those who had attained high ranks or wealth. This is a central theme: his aversion to the powerful and wealthy.

A specific story from his student al-Malali illustrates this: during a desert outing, they saw distant horsemen in fine clothes. When told they were the sultan's elite, Sanusi sought refuge (with God) and turned away. On another occasion, when escape was impossible, he turned his face to the wall and covered it until they passed. This shows his extreme measures to avoid contact with worldly authorities.

Another anecdote involves his completion of a tafsir (Quranic exegesis). He planned to recite Surah al-Ikhlas one day and al-Mu'awwidhatayn another day. When the minister heard and wanted to attend, Sanusi changed his plan and recited all three in one day to avoid the minister's presence. This highlights his fear of association with officials.

The sultan himself requested Sanusi to come and recite the tafsir in his presence, as was customary. Sanusi refused, and despite insistence, he wrote an apology pleading overwhelming shyness. They despaired and left him alone.

His avoidance extended to social events: if he heard of a wedding feast of a worldly person, he would stay away for that day and sometimes distant days, lest he be invited and forced to attend.

Regarding gifts, he absolutely refused those from the sultan or those associated with him. If gifts came to his house in his absence, he would rebuke his family. He accepted gifts only from those with lawful earnings, and he would pray for them. This shows his scrupulousness about the source of wealth.

A remarkable incident: the son of the caliph came with a slave, kissed his hands and feet, and offered a gift. Sanusi refused, then smiled and prayed for him. When asked to take it and give it to the poor, he still refused. This shows his refusal even of charity from such sources.

He was often sought for intercession and writing to amirs. He disliked this, but sometimes did it out of shyness. Once, a man asked him to write thirty letters of acquittal; he wrote them but lamented, 'These are calamities we have been afflicted with.'

His ultimate wish was to see no one and be seen by no one. He said, 'I have no need of anyone or his wealth.'

The passage ends with a poetic tribute by al-Hawdi, praising his rejection of worldly leadership and its allure.

Finally, the text transitions to his worship, devotion, fear of God, and good conduct, but this part is incomplete in our window. The entire passage serves to paint a picture of a scholar who embodied complete ascetic detachment.

العبادة

A) He forgot his original plan.

D) He was asked to do so by his students.

Key Takeaway
Sanusi avoided any situation that might bring him into association with worldly authorities, even changing religious practices to do so.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a time you altered a religious practice because you wanted to avoid being seen with certain people. What does the ease with which you changed it reveal about your heart's attachment to this world? Will you examine your intentions before your next act of worship?
?
When have you attended a social gathering even though you knew it would pull you away from your spiritual goals? What would it cost you to stay away like Sanusi did—not from fear of missing out, but from fear of compromising your faith? Are you willing to miss a wedding feast for the sake of your soul?
?
If a person of authority invited you to share your religious knowledge in a setting that could boost your status, would you refuse like Sanusi? What excuse would you give? Your answer shows where you truly seek honor—from God or from creation. Will you choose the honor that lasts?

Refusal of Gifts and Intercessions
Narrative of Sanusi's strictness regarding gifts, intercessions, and his desire for obscurity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Sanusi's strict policy on gifts and intercessions, and his ultimate wish for anonymity.

Core Idea
Sanusi refused any gift from worldly sources, even in charity, and disliked intercessory roles.

This lesson covers Sanusi's scrupulousness regarding wealth and influence. He rejected gifts from the sultan and his affiliates, accepted only from those with lawful earnings, and reluctantly engaged in intercessions. The passage concludes with a poetic tribute and his wish for complete obscurity.

Guided Path
1
Read about his refusal of gifts
Note that he rejected gifts from the sultan and rebuked his family if they accepted. He accepted only from those with lawful earnings.
2
Examine the incident with the caliph's son
The son offered a gift; Sanusi refused, then even refused to accept it for charity.
This shows his extreme caution.
3
Consider his intercessions and wish
He disliked intercessory letters but sometimes did them out of shyness. He called them 'calamities'. He wished to be unseen.
The poetic tribute affirms his rejection of worldly leadership.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الصالح الحسن أبركان [؟]، فامتنع، فألحوا عليه، فكتب في الاعتذار وكتابة مطولة، فقبل منه. كان لا يأنس بأحدٍ ولا يتسبب في معرفته، وقد تبغض إليه الاجتماع بأهل الدنيا والنظر إليهم وقربهم، وهو لمن بسط لهم فيها ونالوا رتبها .. أشدُّ بغضاً، قال تلميذه الملالي: (خرج يوماً معنا للصحراء، فرأى فرساناً بثياب فاخرة على بعد، فقال: من هؤلاء؟ قلنا: خواص السلطان، فتعوذ ورجع لطريق آخر ولقيهم مرة أخرى، وما تمكن من الرجوع، فجعل وجهه للحائط وغطاه حتى جازوا ولم يروه). ولما أراد ختم التفسير عزم على قراءة سورة (الإخلاص) يوماً و(المعوذتين) يوماً، فسمع به الوزير وأراد حضور الختم فبلغه فقرأ السور الثلاثة يوماً واحداً؛ خيفة حضور الوزير عنده ذلك، ورؤيته والاجتماع به. وطلبه السلطان أن يطلع إليه ويقرأ التفسير بحضرته على عادة المفسرين، فامتنع، فألحوا عليه، فكتب إليه معتذراً بغلبة الحياء له، ولا يقدر على التكلم هناك، فأيسوا منه. وإذا سمع يوماً بوليمة أحد أبناء الدنيا تخلف من يومه عن الحضور؛ خيفة أن يُدعى، فلا يظهرُ بالكلية حتى تمر أيام الوليمة وربما تخلف قبله أياماً. وحاشى نفسه عن قبول عطايا السلطان ومن لاذ به، وربما تأتي داره وهو غائب، فإذا وجدها أنكر على أهل داره، وتغيَّر كثيراً، وكان يقبل هدايا غيرهم ممَّن طابت أكسابهم، ويدعو لهم. وقد جاءه يوماً ابن الخليفة ومعه عبد، فأقبل يقبل يديه ورجليه، وطلب منه قبوله هدية، فأبى، ثم تبسم مطيِّباً قلبه ودعا له، فطلب منه أن يأخذه ويتصدق به على الفقراء، فأبى ولم يرضَ لهم ذلك. ولعله كان يُقصد بشفاعات وقضاء حوائج، ويُطلب منه الكتابة إلى الأمراء، فكان يكره ذلك، وإن فعل فعن حياء؛ فقد كلَّفه إنسان يوماً بكتب ثلاثين براءة، فكتبها وقال: هذه مصائب ابتلينا بها. وكان يتمنَّى ألا يرى أحداً وألا يراه أحدٌ، وكان يقول: لا حاجة لي بأحد ولا بماله. وقد قال فيه العلامة الحوضي: (من الكامل) كم جاءَتِ الدنيا تسوقُ رئاسة يبغي إليك تقـربـاً أبناؤهــا فأبيت عنها معرضاً مستحقراً لم يخدعَنْكَ جمالها وبهاؤها. وأما عبادته وتبتله وخوفه من الله تعالى، وحسن معاملته مع عباده:
Al-Salih al-Hasan Abrakan [؟], so he refused, but they insisted, so he wrote in apology and a lengthy writing, and he accepted from him. He did not feel at ease with anyone and did not cause himself to be known, and he detested associating with people of the world, looking at them, and being near them, and he hated more intensely those who were given worldly goods and attained high ranks. His student al-Malali said: 'One day he went out with us to the desert, and he saw horsemen in luxurious clothing in the distance, and said: Who are these? We said: The sultan's elite. So he sought refuge and returned by another path. He encountered them another time and was not able to turn back, so he turned his face to the wall and covered it until they passed and did not see him.' And when he wanted to complete the tafsir, he resolved to recite Surah al-Ikhlas one day and al-Mu'awwidhatayn another day, but the minister heard of it and wanted to attend the completion ceremony, so when he learned of it, he recited the three surahs in one day, fearing the minister's presence, seeing him, and meeting with him. And the sultan requested that he come to him and recite the tafsir in his presence according to the custom of the exegetes, but he refused, and they insisted, so he wrote to him apologizing that shyness overcame him and he could not speak there, so they despaired of him. And if he heard one day of a wedding feast of one of the worldly people, he would stay away from attending that day, fearing that he might be invited, so he would not appear at all until the days of the feast passed, and sometimes he would stay away days before it. And he refrained from accepting gifts from the sultan and those who sought refuge with him, and sometimes gifts would come to his house while he was absent, and if he found them, he would rebuke his family and become very upset, but he would accept gifts from others whose earnings were lawful, and he would pray for them. And one day the son of the caliph came to him with a slave, and he began kissing his hands and feet, and asked him to accept a gift, but he refused, then he smiled, comforting his heart, and prayed for him, so he asked him to take it and give it in charity to the poor, but he refused and did not accept that for them. And perhaps he was sought for intercessions and fulfilling needs, and asked to write to the amirs, and he disliked that, and if he did it, it was out of shyness; for one day a man tasked him with writing thirty letters of acquittal, so he wrote them and said: These are calamities we have been afflicted with. And he wished that no one would see him and that he would see no one, and he used to say: I have no need of anyone or his wealth. And the scholar al-Hawdi said about him (in the meter kamil): How often the world came, bringing leadership, its sons seeking to draw near to you, but you refused it, turning away, holding it in contempt, its beauty and splendor did not deceive you. And as for his worship, his devotion, his fear of Allah the Exalted, and his good conduct with His servants:

Commentary
This passage is a biographical account of Imam Sanusi's extraordinary asceticism and piety. It proceeds through a series of anecdotes and observations that illustrate his complete detachment from worldly status, power, and social interaction.

The text begins by recounting how he refused something (possibly a request), but after persistent insistence, he wrote a lengthy apology, which was accepted. This sets the tone for his reluctance to engage.

The narrative then states that he did not feel at ease with anyone and actively avoided being known. He detested associating with worldly people, especially those who had attained high ranks or wealth. This is a central theme: his aversion to the powerful and wealthy.

A specific story from his student al-Malali illustrates this: during a desert outing, they saw distant horsemen in fine clothes. When told they were the sultan's elite, Sanusi sought refuge (with God) and turned away. On another occasion, when escape was impossible, he turned his face to the wall and covered it until they passed. This shows his extreme measures to avoid contact with worldly authorities.

Another anecdote involves his completion of a tafsir (Quranic exegesis). He planned to recite Surah al-Ikhlas one day and al-Mu'awwidhatayn another day. When the minister heard and wanted to attend, Sanusi changed his plan and recited all three in one day to avoid the minister's presence. This highlights his fear of association with officials.

The sultan himself requested Sanusi to come and recite the tafsir in his presence, as was customary. Sanusi refused, and despite insistence, he wrote an apology pleading overwhelming shyness. They despaired and left him alone.

His avoidance extended to social events: if he heard of a wedding feast of a worldly person, he would stay away for that day and sometimes distant days, lest he be invited and forced to attend.

Regarding gifts, he absolutely refused those from the sultan or those associated with him. If gifts came to his house in his absence, he would rebuke his family. He accepted gifts only from those with lawful earnings, and he would pray for them. This shows his scrupulousness about the source of wealth.

A remarkable incident: the son of the caliph came with a slave, kissed his hands and feet, and offered a gift. Sanusi refused, then smiled and prayed for him. When asked to take it and give it to the poor, he still refused. This shows his refusal even of charity from such sources.

He was often sought for intercession and writing to amirs. He disliked this, but sometimes did it out of shyness. Once, a man asked him to write thirty letters of acquittal; he wrote them but lamented, 'These are calamities we have been afflicted with.'

His ultimate wish was to see no one and be seen by no one. He said, 'I have no need of anyone or his wealth.'

The passage ends with a poetic tribute by al-Hawdi, praising his rejection of worldly leadership and its allure.

Finally, the text transitions to his worship, devotion, fear of God, and good conduct, but this part is incomplete in our window. The entire passage serves to paint a picture of a scholar who embodied complete ascetic detachment.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?

Is there a role you play—seeking favors, networking for influence, or using your status to get ahead—that you actually resent but continue out of habit or fear? Are you ready to see that role as a spiritual burden and actively step away from it, even if it means losing opportunities?
?
Sanusi wished to be unseen and see no one. In what areas of your life do you crave recognition or attention, and how does that desire pull you away from sincere devotion? What one step can you take this week to seek obscurity and please Allah alone, expecting no human acknowledgment?

The Fear and Humility of Imam Sanusi
Sanusi's inner piety and outer humility described.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Sanusi's deep fear of God manifested in his humility, constant dhikr, and gentle character.

Core Idea
True fear of God produces profound humility and mercy, not harshness.

Imam Sanusi was not only a scholar but a living example of how knowledge of God transforms character. This passage introduces his state of constant awareness of Allah, marked by prolonged sorrow and fear, yet combined with remarkable humility and gentleness. As you read, notice how his inner state with his Lord shaped his outer conduct with people.

Guided Path
1
Read the description of his inner state
Notice the keywords: 'طويل الحزن', 'كثير الخوف', 'أنين' while in dhikr. Reflect on how his fear led to absorption in remembrance.
Fear here is not negative; it is reverence and awe.
2
Identify his outward qualities
The passage lists humility, good character, tenderness, smiling. See how many attributes are mentioned.
3
Observe his interaction with people
He honored elders, stood with the young, humbled to the weak. Notice the balance between fear of God and kindness to people.
4
Reflect on his preaching focus
His preaching centered on fear of God and the afterlife, yet he tailored advice to each person's state.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فكان هذا في إمام زمانه ، وقد أثرت فيه صحبة الصوفية وعلماء الحقائق ، فطاب خبره و مخبره ، وحَسُنت سيرته وسريرته كان رضي الله عنه طويل الحزن ، كثير الخوف ، ولشدة خوفه مع لصدره أنين وهو مستغرق في الذكر ، فلا يشعر بمن معه ، تواضع وحسن خلق ورقة قلب ، رحيماً متبسماً في وجه من لقيه ، مع إقبال وحسن كلام ، يتزاحم الأطفال على تقبيل أطرافه ، ليناً هيناً حتى في مشيه ، ما ترى أحسن خلقاً ، ولا أوسع صدراً ، ولا أكرم نفساً ولا أعطف قلباً ، ولا أحفظ عهداً .. منه ؛ يوفّر الكبير ، ويقف مع الصغير ، ويتواضع للضعفاء ، مُعظَّماً جانب النبوة في غاية ، حتى ارتحل الناس إليه وتبرَّكوا به . لا يعارضه أحد إلا أفحمه ، جمع له العلم والعمل والولاية إلى مع شفقته على الخلق وقضاء حوائجهم عند السلطان ، 6 النهاية والصبر على إذايتهم ، وضع له من القبول والهيبة والإجلال في القلوب ما لم ينله غيره من علماء عصره وزُمَّاده . وكان جل وعظه للناس في الخوف من المولى الجليل ومراقبته ، وذكر أحوال الآخرة ، وهو إلى هذا يعظ كل أحدٍ بما يناسب حاله ، وقل أن تراه إلا وهو يحرّك شفتيه بذكر الله تعالى(۱) ومن صور شفقته وخوفه من مولاه سبحانه : أنه مرَّ به ذئب يطارده صيَّادٌ وكلابُهُ فحبسوه ثم ذبح ، فلما وصل إليه ورآه ملقى على الأرض .. بكى وقال : لا إله إلا الله ، أين الروح التي يجري بها ؟! وكان يقول : ( ينبغي للإنسان أن يمشي برفق وينظر أمامه ؛ لئلا يقتل دابة في الأرض .). وإذا رأى من يضرب دابة ضرباً عنيفاً .. تغيَّرَ ، وقال لضاربها : ارفق يا مبارك ! وكان ينهى مؤدِّبي الكُتاب عن ضرب الصبيان ، ويقول : ( الله تعالى مئة رحمة ، لا مطمع فيها إلا لمن اتّسم برحمة الخلق جميع وأشفق عليهم ) و زاره في مرض موته بعض العلماء وكان قد أساء إليه ، فطلب منه السماح ، فغفر له ودعا له بالخير ، ولما مات الإمام بكى عليه هذا العالم وتألّم جداً وقال : فقدت الدنيا بفقده وكان يتصدَّق ويأمر أهله بالصدقة ، لا سيما أيام الشدة والجوع ، ويقول : ( من أحب الجنة فليكثر الصدقة خصوصاً في الغلاء ) وكان يؤثر الخلوات ، ويزور المواضع الخربة للاعتبار ، ويقول : این سكانها ؟ وكيف يتنعمون ؟
He was like this in the imam of his time, and the company of the Sufis and scholars of realities influenced him, so his reputation and inner state became good, and his conduct and secret were beautiful. God be pleased with him, he was of prolonged sorrow, much fear, and due to the intensity of his fear, his chest had moaning while he was absorbed in dhikr, so he did not perceive those with him. Humility, good character, tenderness of heart, merciful, smiling in the face of whoever met him, with welcome and good speech. Children would crowd to kiss his limbs, soft and gentle even in his walk. You would not see anyone better in character, nor broader in chest, nor nobler in soul, nor more affectionate in heart, nor more faithful to a covenant... than him. He honored the elder, stood with the young, and humbled himself to the weak, magnifying the side of prophethood to the utmost, so people traveled to him and sought blessing from him. No one opposed him but he silenced him. Knowledge, action, and sainthood were combined for him, along with his compassion for creation and fulfilling their needs with the sultan, to the utmost, and patience with their harm. Acceptance, awe, and veneration were placed for him in hearts such as none other of the scholars of his time and his contemporaries attained. And most of his preaching to people was about fear of the majestic Master and watching Him, and mentioning the states of the Hereafter. Yet with this, he preached to everyone according to what suited his state. Rarely did you see him except moving his lips with the remembrance of God Almighty (1). Among the examples of his compassion and fear of his Lord Glorified is: a wolf passed by him which a hunter and his dogs were chasing, so they trapped it and then slaughtered it. When he reached it and saw it thrown on the ground, he wept and said: There is no god but God, where is the soul by which it moves?! He used to say: 'A person should walk gently and look ahead, lest he kill an animal on the ground.' And if he saw someone striking an animal violently, he would change and say to the striker: Be gentle, O blessed one! He used to forbid the teachers of the kuttab from beating boys, and he said: 'God Almighty has one hundred mercies; there is no hope for them except for one who is characterized by mercy for all creation and is compassionate toward them.' Some scholars who had wronged him visited him in his terminal illness, and he asked forgiveness from them, so he forgave him and prayed for good for him. When the imam died, this scholar wept for him and was greatly pained and said: 'I lost the world with his loss.' He used to give charity and command his family to give charity, especially in times of hardship and hunger, and he said: 'Whoever loves Paradise, let him give much charity, especially during times of high prices.' He preferred solitude and visited ruined places for reflection, and said: 'Where are its inhabitants? And how are they enjoying themselves?'
Source pages p. 43

Commentary
This passage is a biographical account that details the exemplary character and spiritual state of Imam Sanusi. It begins by describing him as the imam of his time, influenced by Sufis and scholars of spiritual realities, and notes the harmony between his outer reputation and inner state. The author then lists his qualities: prolonged sorrow and intense fear of God, so much so that his moans were audible while absorbed in dhikr. Despite this, he was humble, kind, and smiling to all, including children who would crowd to kiss him. The text emphasizes his unparalleled character: no one was better in conduct, more open-hearted, nobler in soul, or more compassionate. He honored elders, stood with the young, and humbled himself to the weak, all while magnifying the prophetic heritage. People traveled to him for blessings. He silenced opponents, and combined knowledge, action, and sainthood with compassion for people, using his influence with the sultan to fulfill their needs. His preaching focused on fear of God and the afterlife, tailored to each listener, and he was constantly engaged in dhikr. The passage then gives specific examples: his weeping over a slaughtered wolf, teaching gentleness toward animals, forbidding harsh punishment of children, forgiving a scholar who wronged him, giving charity, and seeking solitude in ruined places for reflection. Each anecdote illustrates his deep fear of God and limitless mercy for all creatures.

Correction: Imam Sanusi's example shows that profound fear is accompanied by humility, gentleness, and compassion.

The Mercy of Imam Sanusi to All Creatures
Specific examples of Imam Sanusi's mercy and compassion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will appreciate how Imam Sanusi's compassion extended to animals, children, and even those who wronged him, as shown through specific anecdotes.

Core Idea
Compassion for all creatures is a sign of true fear of God.

In the second part of the biography, the author illustrates Imam Sanusi's practical mercy through powerful stories. From weeping over a slaughtered wolf to forbidding harsh punishment of children, each example demonstrates that his fear of God translated into active care for every living being. This passage invites us to consider how our own piety should manifest in compassion.

Guided Path
1
Read the wolf anecdote
Notice his reaction: weeping and reflecting on the soul. Consider what this shows about his view of animals.
He didn't just ignore the dead wolf; he pondered its soul.
2
Examine his instruction on walking
He advised walking gently to avoid killing creatures. This shows mindfulness.
3
Observe his stance on children
He forbade beating children and connected mercy to Allah's mercy.
4
Reflect on his forgiveness and charity
He forgave a scholar who wronged him and emphasized charity especially in hard times.
5
Consider his solitude for reflection
He visited ruined places to ponder the transience of life.

فكان هذا في إمام زمانه ، وقد أثرت فيه صحبة الصوفية وعلماء الحقائق ، فطاب خبره و مخبره ، وحَسُنت سيرته وسريرته كان رضي الله عنه طويل الحزن ، كثير الخوف ، ولشدة خوفه مع لصدره أنين وهو مستغرق في الذكر ، فلا يشعر بمن معه ، تواضع وحسن خلق ورقة قلب ، رحيماً متبسماً في وجه من لقيه ، مع إقبال وحسن كلام ، يتزاحم الأطفال على تقبيل أطرافه ، ليناً هيناً حتى في مشيه ، ما ترى أحسن خلقاً ، ولا أوسع صدراً ، ولا أكرم نفساً ولا أعطف قلباً ، ولا أحفظ عهداً .. منه ؛ يوفّر الكبير ، ويقف مع الصغير ، ويتواضع للضعفاء ، مُعظَّماً جانب النبوة في غاية ، حتى ارتحل الناس إليه وتبرَّكوا به . لا يعارضه أحد إلا أفحمه ، جمع له العلم والعمل والولاية إلى مع شفقته على الخلق وقضاء حوائجهم عند السلطان ، 6 النهاية والصبر على إذايتهم ، وضع له من القبول والهيبة والإجلال في القلوب ما لم ينله غيره من علماء عصره وزُمَّاده . وكان جل وعظه للناس في الخوف من المولى الجليل ومراقبته ، وذكر أحوال الآخرة ، وهو إلى هذا يعظ كل أحدٍ بما يناسب حاله ، وقل أن تراه إلا وهو يحرّك شفتيه بذكر الله تعالى(۱) ومن صور شفقته وخوفه من مولاه سبحانه : أنه مرَّ به ذئب يطارده صيَّادٌ وكلابُهُ فحبسوه ثم ذبح ، فلما وصل إليه ورآه ملقى على الأرض .. بكى وقال : لا إله إلا الله ، أين الروح التي يجري بها ؟! وكان يقول : ( ينبغي للإنسان أن يمشي برفق وينظر أمامه ؛ لئلا يقتل دابة في الأرض .). وإذا رأى من يضرب دابة ضرباً عنيفاً .. تغيَّرَ ، وقال لضاربها : ارفق يا مبارك ! وكان ينهى مؤدِّبي الكُتاب عن ضرب الصبيان ، ويقول : ( الله تعالى مئة رحمة ، لا مطمع فيها إلا لمن اتّسم برحمة الخلق جميع وأشفق عليهم ) و زاره في مرض موته بعض العلماء وكان قد أساء إليه ، فطلب منه السماح ، فغفر له ودعا له بالخير ، ولما مات الإمام بكى عليه هذا العالم وتألّم جداً وقال : فقدت الدنيا بفقده وكان يتصدَّق ويأمر أهله بالصدقة ، لا سيما أيام الشدة والجوع ، ويقول : ( من أحب الجنة فليكثر الصدقة خصوصاً في الغلاء ) وكان يؤثر الخلوات ، ويزور المواضع الخربة للاعتبار ، ويقول : این سكانها ؟ وكيف يتنعمون ؟
He was like this in the imam of his time, and the company of the Sufis and scholars of realities influenced him, so his reputation and inner state became good, and his conduct and secret were beautiful. God be pleased with him, he was of prolonged sorrow, much fear, and due to the intensity of his fear, his chest had moaning while he was absorbed in dhikr, so he did not perceive those with him. Humility, good character, tenderness of heart, merciful, smiling in the face of whoever met him, with welcome and good speech. Children would crowd to kiss his limbs, soft and gentle even in his walk. You would not see anyone better in character, nor broader in chest, nor nobler in soul, nor more affectionate in heart, nor more faithful to a covenant... than him. He honored the elder, stood with the young, and humbled himself to the weak, magnifying the side of prophethood to the utmost, so people traveled to him and sought blessing from him. No one opposed him but he silenced him. Knowledge, action, and sainthood were combined for him, along with his compassion for creation and fulfilling their needs with the sultan, to the utmost, and patience with their harm. Acceptance, awe, and veneration were placed for him in hearts such as none other of the scholars of his time and his contemporaries attained. And most of his preaching to people was about fear of the majestic Master and watching Him, and mentioning the states of the Hereafter. Yet with this, he preached to everyone according to what suited his state. Rarely did you see him except moving his lips with the remembrance of God Almighty (1). Among the examples of his compassion and fear of his Lord Glorified is: a wolf passed by him which a hunter and his dogs were chasing, so they trapped it and then slaughtered it. When he reached it and saw it thrown on the ground, he wept and said: There is no god but God, where is the soul by which it moves?! He used to say: 'A person should walk gently and look ahead, lest he kill an animal on the ground.' And if he saw someone striking an animal violently, he would change and say to the striker: Be gentle, O blessed one! He used to forbid the teachers of the kuttab from beating boys, and he said: 'God Almighty has one hundred mercies; there is no hope for them except for one who is characterized by mercy for all creation and is compassionate toward them.' Some scholars who had wronged him visited him in his terminal illness, and he asked forgiveness from them, so he forgave him and prayed for good for him. When the imam died, this scholar wept for him and was greatly pained and said: 'I lost the world with his loss.' He used to give charity and command his family to give charity, especially in times of hardship and hunger, and he said: 'Whoever loves Paradise, let him give much charity, especially during times of high prices.' He preferred solitude and visited ruined places for reflection, and said: 'Where are its inhabitants? And how are they enjoying themselves?'
Source pages p. 43

Commentary
This passage is a biographical account that details the exemplary character and spiritual state of Imam Sanusi. It begins by describing him as the imam of his time, influenced by Sufis and scholars of spiritual realities, and notes the harmony between his outer reputation and inner state. The author then lists his qualities: prolonged sorrow and intense fear of God, so much so that his moans were audible while absorbed in dhikr. Despite this, he was humble, kind, and smiling to all, including children who would crowd to kiss him. The text emphasizes his unparalleled character: no one was better in conduct, more open-hearted, nobler in soul, or more compassionate. He honored elders, stood with the young, and humbled himself to the weak, all while magnifying the prophetic heritage. People traveled to him for blessings. He silenced opponents, and combined knowledge, action, and sainthood with compassion for people, using his influence with the sultan to fulfill their needs. His preaching focused on fear of God and the afterlife, tailored to each listener, and he was constantly engaged in dhikr. The passage then gives specific examples: his weeping over a slaughtered wolf, teaching gentleness toward animals, forbidding harsh punishment of children, forgiving a scholar who wronged him, giving charity, and seeking solitude in ruined places for reflection. Each anecdote illustrates his deep fear of God and limitless mercy for all creatures.

The Daily Devotional Routine of Imam al-Sanusi
Sanusi describes the daily devotional routine of Imam al-Sanusi

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the comprehensive daily spiritual and scholarly routine of Imam al-Sanusi and how it reflects the integrated life of a traditional Islamic scholar.

Core Idea
A scholar's day is a seamless blend of worship, teaching, and spiritual retreat.

Imam al-Sanusi was not only a great theologian but also a model of disciplined spirituality. His daily schedule, described in this passage, shows how he balanced teaching, prayer, Quran recitation, and seclusion. This lesson will walk you through his routine so you can appreciate the harmony between outward service and inward devotion.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its English translation to get a sense of the routine. Notice the sequence of activities and the times of day.
The passage uses specific terms like أوراد (awrad) and خلوات (khalawat).
2
Identify the key components of the routine
List the main activities: after dawn prayer (awrad, teaching), mid-morning (duha prayer with ten hizb), afternoon (study, retreat), evening (supererogatory prayers, Quran), night (sleep, study, prayer until dawn).
3
Reflect on the spiritual principles
Consider why he disliked speaking after certain prayers, why he delayed the takbir, and why he valued seclusion. These show mindfulness and intentionality.
4
Consider the balance of roles
Note how he combined teaching, community interaction, personal worship, and study. This balance is a hallmark of a true scholar.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كان يكره الكلام بعد الصبح والعصر ، ويتراخي في صلاته بتكبيرة الإحرام بعد الإقامة ، ولا يكبر إلا بعد حين ، وكان بعد صلاة الصبح في مسجده يقرأ أوراده ، ثم يباشر بإقراء العلم إلى وقت الفطور المعتاد ، ثم يقف ساعة مع الناس على باب داره ، ثم يدخل ويصلي الضحى قدر قراءة عشرة أحزاب ، ثم يشتغل بالمطالعة نهاراً وكان بعد الزوال يخرج لخلواته إلى الغروب ، وأحياناً يصلي الظهر مع الناس ثم يجلس يصلي ويتنفل ويقرأ إلى العصر ، ثم يشتغل بورده إلى الغروب ، فإذا صلى المغرب صلى ست رکعات وبقي في مسجده حتى يصلي العشاء ، ويقرأ ما تيسر . ثم يرجع إلى داره وينام ساعة ، ثم يشتغل بالنظر أو النسخ ساعة . ثم يصلي إلى طلوع الفجر . فيا لها من أحوال شريفة ، نال صاحبها المقامات المنيفة ! فأكرم به من عالم عامل ، ومنارة هدي تتلمحها أعين القُصَّاد ، وسالكي سبل الرشاد !
He disliked speaking after dawn and afternoon prayer, and he would be unhurried in his prayer with the takbir of the opening (takbirat al-ihram) after the iqama, and he would not say the takbir until after a while. After the dawn prayer (salat al-subh), he would be in his mosque reciting his litanies (awrad), then he would directly occupy himself with teaching knowledge until the usual breakfast time. Then he would stand for an hour with the people at his house door, then he would enter and pray the forenoon prayer (al-duha) to the extent of reciting ten hizb. Then he would busy himself with reading (mutala'a) during the day. After noon (al-zawal), he would go out for his retreats (khalawat) until sunset. Sometimes he would pray the noon prayer (al-zuhr) with the people, then sit praying supererogatory prayers and reciting until the afternoon prayer (al-'asr). Then he would busy himself with his wird (litany) until sunset. When he prayed the sunset prayer (al-maghrib), he would pray six rak'at and remain in his mosque until he prayed the night prayer (al-'isha), and he would recite whatever was easy. Then he would return to his house and sleep for an hour. Then he would occupy himself with looking (nazar) or copying (naskh) for an hour. Then he would pray until the break of dawn. What noble states these are! Their possessor attained lofty stations! How generous is he, a learned and active scholar, a beacon of guidance that is sought by the eyes of those who seek and those who traverse the paths of right guidance!
Source pages p. 46

Commentary
This passage describes the daily routine of Imam al-Sanusi, illustrating his dedication to worship, teaching, and spiritual discipline. It begins by noting his dislike for speaking after the dawn and afternoon prayers, indicating a preference for silence and reflection. He would delay the opening takbir after the iqama, showing deliberateness in prayer. After dawn prayer, he would recite his awrad (litanies) in his mosque, then immediately engage in teaching until breakfast. He would then greet people for an hour at his doorstep, demonstrating humility and accessibility. He would then pray duha, a supererogatory forenoon prayer, reciting ten hizb of Quran. He spent the rest of the day in study (mutala'a). After noon, he would go into seclusion (khalwa) until sunset, sometimes praying zuhr with the congregation and then continuing in supererogatory prayer and Quran recitation until asr. He would then focus on his wird until maghrib. After maghrib, he would pray six rak'at and remain in the mosque until isha, reciting Quran. He would return home, sleep for an hour, then spend an hour in study or copying, and finally pray until dawn. The author concludes with a praise of these noble states, emphasizing that such a person has attained high stations and is a guiding light for seekers.

Some Miracles (Karamat) of Imam al-Sanusi
Sanusi recounts two miracles of Imam al-Sanusi, prefacing them with a note on their relation to his scholarly rank.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the miracles of Imam al-Sanusi are intimately connected to his scholarly station and his mastery of kalam, and you will appreciate how his contemplation of creation reflects his theological reasoning.

Core Idea
Imam al-Sanusi's miracles blend seamlessly with his science of kalam.

This passage from the biography of Imam al-Sanusi offers us glimpses of his spiritual state through two miracles (karamat). The author cautions us not to focus solely on the miraculous, but to recognize that the Imam's greatest gift is his scholarly rank. Yet, even these extraordinary events are intertwined with the very science of kalam that he mastered. As you read, notice how contemplation of the natural world becomes a form of theological reflection.

Guided Path
1
Read the preface carefully
The author begins by downplaying the importance of miracles compared to scholarly rank. Notice the phrase 'لا يعظم شأنه بحكاية كرامة له' — his status is not magnified by relating miracles. Then observe how he pivots to say that relating them is still valuable, especially because they are blended with kalam.
2
Explore the dream miracle
The first miracle involves a dream in which the Imam's deceased father says he was forgiven because of his son's contemplation of a mountain at the time of burial. Reflect on how this contemplation is itself an act of kalam — reflecting on creation to understand the Creator.
3
Examine the incomplete meat miracle
The second miracle is cut off, but we see the beginning: a man carrying meat enters the mosque fearing he will miss a rak'a. Consider what might happen next and how this relates to the Imam's karamat.
The story is incomplete; the original text likely continues on the next page.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
طرف من كراماته مثلُ الإمام السنوسي لا يعظم شأنه بحكاية كرامة له ؛ إذ حسبنا ما أكرمه الله به حين بواه هذه المنزلة العلمية الرفيعة ، وأقامه عَلَمَ هدىً لإرشاد الخلق ، واستنقذ بما أجراه الله على يراعه كثيراً من التائهين المتحيّرين ، ولكن حكاية ذلك من باب زيادة العناية والتّعظيم ومن اللطيف أن ترى له بعض الكرامات ممزوجةً بعلم الكلام ! وهذا دال على صدق حال الإمام مع الله هذا العلم المقرب من تعالى ؛ فقد حكى العلامة الشفشاوني عن بعض الأولياء أنه رأى والد الشيخ السنوسي بعد موته في المنام ، فقال له : ما فعل الله بك ؟ فقال : غفر لي ، فقال : بم ؟ قال : بتفكر ولدي في الجبل ساعة دفني ! فلمَّا سُئل الإمام السنوسي عن ذلك قال : نعم ؛ كنت أتفكر في الجبل الذي كان أمامي ؛ وهو المطل على تلمسان ، وكم فيه من جواهر ، وكيف ركبه الحكيم بقدرته وحكمته ومن كراماته : أنَّ رجلاً اشترى لحماً من السوق ، فسمع الإقامة في المسجد ، فدخل واللحم معه يحمله ، فخاف من طرحه فوات ركعة
Some of his miracles (karamat). Imam al-Sanusi is not such that his standing is magnified by relating a miracle of his; it is sufficient for us what Allah honored him with when He granted him this lofty scholarly station, and set him up as a beacon of guidance for guiding creation, and through what Allah caused to flow from his pen, He rescued many of the lost and bewildered. But relating that is a means of increased care and veneration. It is a subtle point that you see some of his miracles blended with the science of kalam! And this indicates the truthfulness of the Imam's state with Allah regarding this science that draws near to the Exalted. Indeed, the scholar al-Shifshawni related from some of the saints that he saw the father of Shaykh al-Sanusi after his death in a dream, and he said to him: 'What did Allah do with you?' He said: 'He forgave me.' He said: 'By what?' He said: 'By my son's contemplation of the mountain at the hour of my burial!' When Imam al-Sanusi was asked about that, he said: 'Yes; I was contemplating the mountain that was in front of me, which overlooks Tlemcen, and how many jewels are in it, and how the Wise Creator composed it with His power and wisdom.' And among his miracles: a man bought meat from the market, then he heard the iqama in the mosque, so he entered carrying the meat, and he feared that if he threw it away he would miss a rak'a.
Source pages p. 47

Commentary
This passage presents some of the miracles (karamat) of Imam al-Sanusi. It begins with a preface stating that the Imam's status is not dependent on relating miracles; rather, his scholarly rank and his role as a guide are sufficient. However, relating miracles is a way of showing additional care and veneration. The author finds it delightful that some of his miracles are intertwined with the science of kalam, indicating his sincerity and nearness to Allah. The first miracle is a dream narrated by al-Shifshawni: a saint saw the Imam's deceased father, who said he was forgiven because of his son's contemplation of the mountain at the time of burial. The Imam confirmed this, explaining that he was indeed contemplating the mountain overlooking Tlemcen, reflecting on its composition and the wisdom of the Creator. The second miracle is incomplete: a man bought meat, heard the iqama, entered the mosque carrying the meat, and feared missing a rak'a if he threw it away. The story likely concludes on the next page. These miracles demonstrate the Imam's spiritual state and his connection with kalam.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that karamat are the measure of a saint's rank.
Correction: The text emphasizes that the Imam's scholarly station is sufficient; miracles are additional but not the foundation of his status.

?

Miracles Interwoven with Kalam
Sanusi's biographer recounts a miracle linked to kalam: a dream revealing that his father's forgiveness came from his son's mountain reflection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi's personal miracle of reflection on a mountain illustrates the deep connection between kalam (theology) and contemplation of creation, and how a miracle can be a token of divine favor for a scholar of kalam.

Core Idea
Sanusi's miracles affirm that kalam is a path to divine nearness.

Sanusi's biographer begins by noting that his stature is not from miracles but from Allah's gift of knowledge. However, he then observes that some of his miracles are interwoven with kalam. This lesson focuses on one such miracle: a dream in which Sanusi's father is forgiven because of his son's reflection on a mountain. The story shows how theological contemplation can earn divine mercy and reward even beyond the grave.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the biographer's careful framing: he downplays miracles before presenting one.
The biographer uses the phrase 'من باب زيادة العناية والتعظيم' (by way of additional care and veneration).
2
Analyze the first miracle
The dream story: the father is forgiven because of his son's (Sanusi's) reflection (تفكر) in the mountain at the time of burial. Identify what Sanusi was reflecting on and how this relates to kalam.
Sanusi was contemplating the mountain's composition, the gems within, and the power and wisdom of the Creator.
3
Reflect on the lesson
Consider why the biographer highlights that this miracle is mixed with kalam. How does this story elevate the study of kalam as a means of drawing near to Allah?

طرف من كراماته
مثلُ الإمام السنوسي لا يعظم شأنه بحكاية كرامة له ؛ إذ حسبنا ما أكرمه الله به حين بواه هذه المنزلة العلمية الرفيعة ، وأقامه عَلَمَ هدىً لإرشاد الخلق ، واستنقذ بما أجراه الله على يراعه كثيراً من التائهين المتحيّرين ، ولكن حكاية ذلك من باب زيادة العناية والتعظيم.
ومن اللطيف أن ترى له بعض الكرامات ممزوجةً بعلم الكلام ! وهذا دال على صدق حال الإمام مع الله هذا العلم المقرب من تعالى ؛ فقد حكى العلامة الشفشاوني عن بعض الأولياء أنه رأى والد الشيخ السنوسي بعد موته في المنام ، فقال له : ما فعل الله بك ؟ فقال : غفر لي ، فقال : بم ؟ قال : بتفكر ولدي في الجبل ساعة دفني ! فلمَّا سُئل الإمام السنوسي عن ذلك قال : نعم ؛ كنت أتفكر في الجبل الذي كان أمامي ؛ وهو المطل على تلمسان ، وكم فيه من جواهر ، وكيف ركبه الحكيم بقدرته وحكمته.
ومن كراماته : أنَّ رجلاً اشترى لحماً من السوق ، فسمع الإقامة في المسجد ، فدخل واللحم معه يحمله ، فخاف من طرحه فوات ركعة ، وكبر على هذه الحال ، فلما سلَّم وعاد إلى أهله طبخ أهله اللحم إلى صلاة العشاء فلم ينضج ، فأرادوا طرحه فإذا هو على حاله بدمه ، فقالوا : لعله لحم شارف ، فباتوا يوقدون عليه إلى الصبح ، فلم يتغيَّر عن حاله ! فتذكر الرجل ، فذهب إلى الإمام فأعلمه بذلك ، فقال له : يا بني ؛ أرجو الله تعالى أنَّ كل من صلَّى ورائي لا تعدو عليه النار ، ولعل هذا اللحم كان معك حين صليت معي ، ولكن اكتم ذلك.
ذلك : أنه كان يقول ثقةً بمولاه : ( من كانت له إلى الله حاجة فليتوسل بنا وليقدمنا ) ، فرُوي أنَّ امرأة ضاع منها مفتاح بيتها ، فحاولت بكل حيلة فتحه فلم تفلح ، فتوسلت بالشيخ وبجاهه عند ربه ، ففتح الباب وانحل القفل.
ومن كراماته : تأليفه للشرح الكبير لـ « الحوفية » ، الذي سماه : المقرب المستوفي » ، ألفه وهو ابن تسع عشرة سنة ، فلما وقف عليه الشيخ القطب الحسن أبركان . . تعجب منه ، وأمر بإخفائه حتى يكمل مُؤلّفه أربعين سنة ؛ لئلا يصاب بالعين ، ويقول له : لا نظير له فيما أعلم ، ودعا لمؤلفه.
Part of his miracles: The likes of Imam al-Sanusi are not magnified by recounting a miracle of his; indeed sufficient for us is what Allah honored him with when He granted him this high scholarly station, and appointed him a banner of guidance to direct creation, and rescued through what Allah caused to flow from his pen many of the wandering bewildered; but recounting that is by way of additional care and veneration. And it is subtle that you see some of his miracles intermingled with the science of kalam! This indicates the truthfulness of the Imam's state with Allah regarding this science that brings one near to the Exalted; for the scholar al-Shafshawani narrated from some of the saints that he saw the father of Shaykh al-Sanusi after his death in a dream, and said to him: What did Allah do with you? He said: He forgave me. He said: By what? He said: By my son's reflection in the mountain at the hour of my burial! So when Imam al-Sanusi was asked about that, he said: Yes; I was reflecting on the mountain that was before me—it overlooks Tlemcen—and how many gems are in it, and how the Wise One composed it with His power and wisdom. And among his miracles: A man bought meat from the market, and heard the iqama in the mosque, so he entered while carrying the meat, and feared that dropping it would cause him to miss a rak'a, and he said the takbir in this state. When he finished the salutation and returned to his family, his family cooked the meat until the evening prayer but it did not cook. They wanted to throw it away, but it was in its state with its blood. They said: Perhaps it is old meat. So they kept kindling fire on it until morning, but it did not change from its state! So the man remembered, and went to the Imam and informed him of that. He said: O my son; I hope from Allah the Exalted that everyone who prays behind me, the fire will not overcome him; perhaps this meat was with you when you prayed with me, but keep that secret. That: he used to say, trusting in his Lord: "Whoever has a need from Allah, let him seek intercession through us and put us forward." It is narrated that a woman lost the key to her house, and she tried every trick to open it but failed. So she sought intercession through the Shaykh and his standing with his Lord, and the door opened and the lock came undone. And among his miracles: his authorship of the great commentary on al-Hawfiyya, which he named al-Muqrib al-Mustawfi; he wrote it when he was nineteen years old. When Shaykh al-Qutb al-Hasan Abarkan came across it, he marveled at it, and ordered it to be hidden until its author completed forty years, lest he be struck by the evil eye, and he said to him: There is no peer for it to my knowledge, and he made supplication for its author.
Source pages p. 47

Commentary
This passage is a hagiographical section recounting several miracles (karamat) attributed to Imam Muhammad al-Sanusi, as well as one of his early scholarly achievements. The author begins by stating that Imam al-Sanusi's stature does not depend on miracles, but that relating them serves to increase respect and honor. Then he notes that some of these miracles are mixed with the science of kalam, showing his sincerity in pursuing this science. The first story: A saint saw al-Sanusi's father in a dream after his death; the father said Allah forgave him because of his son's reflection on a mountain at the time of his burial. Al-Sanusi confirmed that he was indeed contemplating the mountain's composition by divine power and wisdom. The second story: A man entered the mosque carrying raw meat, prayed behind al-Sanusi, then the meat would not cook despite long boiling and burning; al-Sanusi interpreted this as a sign that those who pray behind him are safe from hellfire, and told the man to conceal the incident. The third: Al-Sanusi used to teach people to seek intercession (tawassul) through him. A woman who lost her key tried everything and then sought his intercession; the door opened miraculously. The fourth: At age nineteen, al-Sanusi authored a commentary on al-Hawfiyya called al-Muqrib al-Mustawfi. The spiritual master al-Hasan Abarkan, upon seeing it, ordered it hidden until the author reached forty to protect him from the evil eye, praising it as without peer.

Key Terms
كرامات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Miracles are the primary basis for a scholar's authority and sanctity.
Correction: The biographer explicitly states that Sanusi's stature is from Allah granting him high scholarly station, not from miracles. Miracles are secondary tokens of divine favor.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what did Sanusi say he was reflecting on at the time of his father's burial?
A) The beauty of the sky
B) The mountain, its gems, and how the Wise Creator composed it
C) The meaning of a Quranic verse
D) The names of Allah

Key Takeaway
Contemplation of the natural world as a form of kalam can be a means of divine mercy, even benefiting others beyond the grave.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever truly reflected on a natural scene—like a mountain, a tree, or the sky—as an act of worship? If not, what prevents you from doing so today? Commit to a specific time this week to contemplate Allah's creation with the intention of drawing near to Him.
?
Imam al-Sanusi's simple act of reflection at his father's burial brought divine mercy. What seemingly small act of worship could you do today that might have spiritual impact beyond what you imagine? Will you do it now?
?
If your greatest legacy were your knowledge and reflection, not your achievements or miracles, would you be satisfied? What are you building that will benefit others even after you are gone?

Life of Imam al-Sanusi
beginner
4 min
Miracles of Protection and Intercession
Stories of a miraculous meat that would not cook and a locked door opening through tawassul.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand two miracles of Sanusi: one showing that his prayer protects his followers from hellfire, and another demonstrating the efficacy of tawassul (seeking intercession) through him.

Core Idea
Sanusi's miracles teach that connection to a saint brings divine protection and intercession.

This lesson presents two practical miracles from Sanusi's life. The first involves a man who prayed behind Sanusi while carrying raw meat; the meat would not cook despite hours of boiling and burning, signifying that those who pray behind Sanusi are safe from hellfire. The second story recounts a woman who, after losing her key, sought intercession through Sanusi and found her door miraculously open. Both illustrate the spiritual power of association with a saint.

Guided Path
1
Read both stories
Read the Arabic passage containing both miracles, then read the translation. Notice the different types of intercession: one through prayer behind the saint, the other through explicit tawassul.
The meat story emphasizes protection, while the key story emphasizes help in need.
2
Understand the meat miracle
Analyze why the meat did not cook. Sanusi explains that the fire did not affect it because the man had prayed behind him, and he hopes that everyone who prays behind him will be safe from the fire of Hell. The meat is a physical sign of that spiritual protection.
Sanusi ordered the man to keep the matter secret, showing humility.
3
Understand the tawassul story
The woman uses Sanusi's formula for tawassul: 'Whoever has a need from Allah, let him seek intercession through us and put us forward.' She does so, and the lock opens. This shows that tawassul is permissible and effective.
The story validates the practice of seeking intercession through righteous individuals.
